\lx a
\ph a
\ps CONN
\sn
\ge SCLS
\is 9.2.5.2
\de subordinate clause marker
\rf Tape:1-1-004
\xv Fɔyɔ wulutɔ a, hɔ kɩtɩba ba mɩ.
\xe If you go to town, buy me some groundnuts.
\xv Ɔtsanɩ fɔyɔ wulutɔ a, hɔ kɩtɩba ba mɩ.
\xe When you go to town, buy me some groundnuts.
\dt 10/Jan/2017

\lx a-
\hm 1
\ph a-
\ps CLASS
\sn
\ge PL
\is 8.1.3
\de plural prefix for noun class 0-/a-, ɩ-/a- (inanimate), ɔ-/a- (human)
\np For ɩ-/a- and ɔ-/a- noun classes there is no difference in tone between the singular and plural. For the 0-/a- noun class, when the singular contains only low tone, the a- prefix causes a high tone on the first syllable after the prefix. For example, "spider" Singular: natɩ [natɪʔ(L)] Plural: anatɩ [anátɪ̂ʔ(L)]
\xv ibwi, abwi
\xe stone, stones
\rf Tape:1-1-018
\xv kufa ɔkʋlɛ, akufa anyɔ
\xe one sheep, two sheep
\dt 29/Aug/2016

\lx -a
\hm 1
\ph -a
\ps CNJ
\sn
\ge and
\is 9.6.1.1
\de and; joins two subjects
\ng attaches to pronoun to give meaning me+and you+and etc
\xv Mɩa nyebi amʋ legyi.
\xe I and the children ate.
\rf Tape:1-1-013
\xv Akua mʋa Kofi bɔ́yɔ sukuu.
\xe Akua, she and Kofi will go to school.
\xv Mɩa fʋ nyɔ abɔ́flɔɩ kpoli.
\xe You and I both will husk maize.
\xv Mɩa mʋ nyɔ abɔ́flɔɩ kpoli.
\xe He and I both with husk maize.
\xv Mɩa mlɩ nyɔ abɔ́flɔɩ kpoli.
\xe You(pl.) and I both will husk maize.
\xv Mɩa Ataa nyɔ abɔ́flɔɩ kpoli.
\xe Ataa and I both will husk maize.
\xv Mɩa Kofi pʋ́ Ataa nyɔ abɔ́flɔɩ kpoli.
\xe Kofi, Ataa and I all will husk maize.
\xv Mɩa amʋ́ nyɔ abɔ́flɔɩ kpoli.
\xe They and I both will husk maize.
\xv Fʋa mʋ nyɔ mlɔ́flɔɩ kpoli.
\xe You and he both will husk maize.
\xv Fʋa amʋ́ nyɔ mlɔ́flɔɩ kpoli.
\xe You and they both will husk maize.
\rf Tape:4-2-460
\xv Kofi mʋa Kwame bɔ́yɔ ntsutso.
\xe Kofi, he and Kwame will go to the water source.
\xv Nyankpʋ mʋ́a onyelulu totswie.
\xe Rain and dew fall.
\cf pʋ́
\dt 12/Jan/2017

\lx abawa
\ph abawá
\ps n
\pl abawafɔ
\plph abawáfɔ
\sn
\ge maid
\is 2.6.5.2
\is 4.5.4.3
\de maidservant; young serving girl
\nt Nkonya equivalent: ɔha wa otsiapʋ
\ns This is a Akan loan word. It comes from "Ɔbea" meaning girl or woman and the suffix wa meaning little or less in value.
\bwl Akan
\bwf abawa
\bwc Tone:LLH - maid servant
\xv Abawa akʋ bʋtowun asʋn amʋ́ awie wa, tsufɛ awie amʋ akʋ bʋbʋ owuntɔlɩ.
\xe Some maidservants suffer under their mistress, because some of the mistress are cruel.
\dt 23/May/2017

\lx adabi
\ph adábi
\ps n
\sn
\ge appellation
\is 3.5.1.7
\is 9.6.3.8
\de appellation for drumming
\na To call the appelation of one of various spiritual entities, e.g., ɔkpɩ, akunta, suma is to invoke its power.
\na The language of drumming is usually Akan. They are proverbs. Each clan has one which is used to summon the clan. Elders also have them. As with the Dagombas drummers at a funeral will drum appelations of passing elders in hopes of collecting a dash.
\rf Tape:1-1-111
\xv Nɩ bʋdɛ atumpa da, ɔhandɛn ɔkʋ dɛtsʋn a, bʋtɛda mʋ adabi pʋ tɩ mʋ.
\xe If they are beating the talking drums and an elder is passing by, they beat his appellation and call him.
\dt 12/Jan/2017

\lx atsulii
\ph at͡sulîː
\ps n
\sn
\ge mushroom
\is 1.5.4
\is 6.2.5.3
\de mushroom
\xv Bʋtɔpʋ atsulii lɛ oputsu, mʋ́ oputsu tɔwa ɔdwɛ. Ɩkʋ tɔkwɛ ɔsʋlʋsʋ, ɩkʋ ɛ tɔkwɛ ɔbɛ oyi ánɩ́ bapuli mʋ́ tswɩ, laplɔ pʋ oyi plɔhɛsʋ. Bʋtegyi kʋ, ɩkʋ ɛ bʋtamagyi.
\xe If they take mushrooms and put them in soup the soup is sweet. Some sprout from the ground, some grow on palm trees that they have uprooted and are lying down and have rotted, and other rotting trees. Some are eaten and some are not eaten.
\ec alternate form atsuwuli LLHL (cf atsuguleʔ Gikyode)
\rf Tape:1-2-125
\rf Tape:1-2-145
\xv Atsulii gyihɛ amʋ kʋ aná gyi:
\xe Some kinds of edible mushrooms are:
\rf Tape:1-2-150
\xv Afio, mʋ́ nwun lɛtɛɩ. Mʋ́ oyi bʋswɩ. Ɩtɔwa ɔdwɛ oputsutɔ. Bʋtɛkaka mʋ́ wa oputsutɔ. Ɩtɔkwɛ sinsʋ.
\xe Afio, it's head is wide. It's stalk is long. It makes soup sweet. They chop it up into soup. It grows in a termite mound.
\rf Tape:1-2-171
\xv Mpionkɛntɛ tɔkwɛ ɔbɛ oyi plɔhɛsʋ. Mʋ́ nwun lɛtɛɩ. Mʋ́ oyi ma swɩ. Fɛtalɩ kpa mʋ́ nwun amʋ kwɛ mʋ́ wa oputsutɔ, si mʋ́ oyi amʋ. Mʋ́ ɛ bʋ ɔdwɛ.
\xe Mpionkɛntɛ sprouts on rotten palm trees. It's head is wide. It's stalk is not long. You can remove the heads and grind them and put them in the soup and leave the stalks. It also is sweet.
\rf Tape:1-2-178
\xv Deyi asʋ tɔkwɛ oyi plɔhɛsʋ. Fɛtalɩ kwɛ mʋ́ wa oputsutɔ. Igyi putuputu, ɩlofuli. Ɩbʋ ɔdwɛ abɛ oputsutɔ.
\xe Monkey ears grows in rotten trees. You can grind it and put it in soup. It is small and white. It is sweet in palm nut soup.
\xv Atsulii bibi tɔkwɛ oyi plɔhɛsʋ. Ɩlebi. Fɛtalɩ kwɛ mʋ́ wa oputsutɔ, tsufɛ igyi putuputu. Mʋ́ oputsu bʋ ɔdwɛ.
\xe Black mushrooms grow in rotting trees, they are small. You can grind them and put them in soup. Their soup is sweet.
\rf Tape:1-2-186
\rf Tape:1-2-194
\xv Mantɛpʋ tɔkwɛ ɔsʋlʋsʋ. Fɛtalɩ kwɛ mʋ́ wa oputsutɔ, mʋ́ ɛ bʋ ɔdwɛ.
\xe Mantɛpo grows in the ground. You can grind it for soup. It too is sweet.
\rf Tape:1-2-200
\xv Kikidiki tɔkwɛ ɔsʋlʋsʋ. Mʋ́ nwun lɛtɛɩ. Mʋ́ oyi bʋ swɩ. Ɩbʋ ɔlɩn, tamɛ afio oyi bʋ swɩ dʋn mʋ́. Bʋtɛkaka mʋ́ wa oputsutɔ.
\xe Kikidiki grows in the ground. Its head is wide and its stalk is long. It has roots but afio stalk is longer than it. They cut them up for soup.
\rf Tape:1-2-210
\xv Abi atsuli tɔkwɛ ɔsʋlʋsʋ. Nɩ fɔwa ndɔ ogya a, ɩtɔkɔkwɛ nʋ. Fɛtalɩ kwɛ mʋ́ wa oputsutɔ. Mʋ́ oputsu bʋ ɔdwɛ.
\xe Seed mushrooms grow in the ground. If you set fire to the farm it sprouts. You can grind them and put them in soup. Its soup is good.
\rf Tape:1-2-217
\xv Kplokplobi tɔkwɛ oyi plɔhɛsʋ. Ɩbʋ ɔplɩ, bʋtamakwɛ mʋ́. Mʋ́a abɛ oputsu bʋ ɔdwɛ dʋbɩ.
\xe Kploklobi grows in rotting trees, It is slippery; they never grind it. It is very good with palm nut soup.
\cf abi atsulii
\cf afio
\cf atsulii bibi
\cf deyi asʋ
\cf kikidiki
\cf kplokplobi
\cf mantɛpʋ
\cf mpionkɛntɛ
\bb Stewart List:0872
\dt 12/Jan/2017

\lx Bumbula
\ph bumbula
\ps PropN
\sn
\ge Bumbula
\is 9.7.2.3
\de town on the main road; five km north of Wurupong
\ee located at 7° 13' 48"N, +0° 20' 23"E
\na This town is named after Buhbler, a German plantation owner. When he was forced to leave because of World War I, the Nkonyas refused to buy back the land, which they considered to be theirs. Ewe settlers from Kpando purchased the land, and it is now a primarily Ewe town, under the Nkonya Wurupong paramountcy.

\lx Ɩpwɛ
\ph ɪpʷɛ
\ps TIME
\sn
\ge 1st day
\is 4.9.7.7
\is 8.4.1.3.1
\de day of rest in Nkonya week; first day of Nkonya week
\na The Nkonya week has six days. The main days are the first and fourth. The first day is Ɩpwɛ, the day of the main fetish Sia.The fourth is Kulihɛ, the day of the ancestors. Various activities, such as funerals, are allowed on certain days but not on others. Farming is not allowed on Ɩpwɛ.
\na In the Southern dialect the days are called "Ɩpwɛ, Ɩpwɛ Odekeyikɛ, Ɩpwɛ Ɔdɛkɛtrakɛ, Kulihɛ, Kulihɛ Odekeyikɛ, Kulihɛ Ɔdɛkɛtrakɛ". In the Northern Dialect they are called "Ɩpwɛ, Ɩpwɛ Odekeyikɛ. Yinke, Kulihɛ, Kulihɛ Odekeyikɛ, Ɔdɛkɛtrakɛ"
\na The (folk) etymology says that the name of the day derives from "pwɛ" because people would stay at home and sculpt things.
\cf pwɛ
\cf Ɩpwɛ Odekeyikɛ
\cf Ɩpwɛ Ɔdɛkɛtrakɛ
\cf Yinke
\cf Kulihɛ
\cf Kulihɛ Odekeyikɛ
\cf Kulihɛ Ɔdɛkɛtrakɛ
\cf Ɔdɛkɛtrakɛ
\dt 17/Oct/2016

\lx Ɩpwɛ Odekeyikɛ
\ph ɪpʷɛ odekeyikɛ
\ps TIME
\sn
\ge Second day
\is 4.9.7.7
\is 8.4.1.3.1
\de the day following Ɩpwɛ; the second day of the Nkonya week
\cf Ɩpwɛ
\dt 20/Jul/2016

\lx Ɩpwɛ Ɔdɛkɛtrakɛ
\ph ɪpʷɛ ɔdɛkɛtrákɛ
\ps TIME
\vad Yinke
\ve Northern
\sn
\ge Third day
\is 4.9.7.7
\is 8.4.1.3.1
\de the second day following Ɩpwɛ; the third day of the Nkonya week
\na See Ɩpwɛ for a list of the Nkonya days.
\ns The Southern dialect has two days called Ɔdɛkɛtrakɛ, Ɩpwɛ Ɔdɛkɛtrakɛ, two days following Ɩpwɛ and Kulihɛ Ɔdɛkɛtrakɛ, two days following Kulihɛ.
\cf Ɩpwɛ
\dt 12/Dec/2016

\lx kwɩɩda
\ph kwɪ̌ːdâʔ
\ps n
\sn
\ge day name
\is 8.4.1.3.1
\de the name given based on what day of the week one is born
\na A person has an affinity for those born on the same day of the week. They are considered to have a similar personality, and can call each other opio (sibling).
\na The day names are Akan loans used widely throughout Southern Ghana. Akans have a seven day traditional week; Nkonyas have a six day traditional week.

\lx -nsa
\ph nsa
\ps NUM
\sn
\ge three
\is 8.1.1.1
\de three, variant form
\cf asa2
\ng The -nsa form of asa occurs with certain nouns with n- plural markers. For example, "nkɛnsa- three days". This form is an example of noun concord in noun adjective combinations. This concord is not common in Nkonya, but other Guang have it more extensively. It occurs only with numbers, and only with the numbers 2 through 7, i. e. -nyɔ, -nsa, -nna, -nnu, -nsie, -nsienɔ.
\np The +ATR forms (nyɔ, -nnu, -nsie, -nsienɔ) cause the word to which they are attached to become +ATR. For example, nkɛ + nsie "days" + "six" becomes nkesie "six days".
\bb Stewart List:1803
\dt 13/Jan/2017

\lx nyankpʋkpie
\ph ɲaŋ͡mk͡pʊ́k͡píeʔ(L)
\ps n
\sn 1
\ge thunder
\is 1.1.3.6
\de thunder
\rf Tape:3-2-537
\xv Nyankpʋ amʋ dekpie dʋbɩ, fialɩ obotswie.
\xe It is thundering a lot, perhaps it will rain.
\ps n
\sn 2
\ge scorpion
\is 1.6.1.8
\de small brown scorpion
\sc Order: Scorpiones
\na The myth is that a scorpion bite will cause pain until the next time you hear thunder;
\ee In the Northern dialect this is any scorpion, in the southern this is only the big black ones. The little brown ones are aganɛ, borrowed from Ewe. Nyankpʋ kpie is cognate with other Guang languages.
\rf Tape:5-1-542
\xv Nyankpʋkpie ɔsʋra fʋ a, ɩbʋ ɔsɩn.
\xe If a scorpion stings you it is painful.

\xv Butofwi ɩbɛ, mɛnɩ nta amʋ bɛ́paɩ.
\xe They smoke palm trees so that the wine will ferment.
\xv Nɩ fɔ́bwɛ obia a, fɔ́bɔbɔɩ awaimbi putuputi, lɛ mʋ́tɔ gyo tsitsa, fɩta mʋ́ fɛ nkɛnsa. Ɩwʋlɩ a, fakpa mʋ́ pʋ́ ɔblakwɛ klɩ mʋ́ alɩ ánɩ́ bowie ɔbɛkpʋn amʋtɔ, fówun fapʋsʋta ogya. Ɩtsankɩ a, fapʋ yofwi ɩbɛ.
\xe If you want to make a torch, you split the central stalk of a palm branch into small pieces, removing and throwing away the core(which is soft). Then you dry the small pieces for three days (or so). When they are dry you gather them and tie them with the new leaves of a palm tree so that they go into the hole in the felled palm tree. You put one end of it in the fire to catch fire and then you put it into the hole to smoke the tree (so that the wine ferments).
\bb Stewart List:1267
\dt 21/Jul/2016

\lx ɔdanta
\hm 1
\ph ɔdántá
\ps n
\pl ndanta
\plph ndántá
\sn
\ge loincloth
\is 5.3.3
\de loincloth
\na In the older days they took the bark of the poli tree and beat it to remove the outer layers and used the inner flexible layers to make cloth and mats.
\na so Igna suggest that the etymology here is pounded cloth ɔda ɔtan
\xv Atɩtɩfɔ bʋtɛda poli afwɛ bwɛ ɔdanta pʋ́ ɔklan.
\xe The olden times people beat poli bark to make loincloths and mat.
\dt 19/Jul/2016

\ph ɔt͡sɔpɪ́
\ps n
\pl ntsɔpɩ
\plph nt͡sɔpɪ́
\sn
\ge idol
\is 4.9.8.1
\de a small clay image sculpted with a mouth that is fed a portion of the food whenever the elder eats
\xv Ɔtsɔpɩ bʋ ansibi, tamɛ ɔtamawun atɔ. Ɔbʋ asʋ, tamɛ ɔtamanu asʋn. Ɔbʋ ayabi, tamɛ ɔtamanatɩ.
\xe Idol has eyes, but does not see. It has ears, but does hear. It has legs, but does not walk.
\nt Usually a child is delegated to feed the idol. Before he does so he will have to use a stick to remove previous portions. Unless he is lucky enough to have a chicken eat it before. It is kept in the corner of a room.

\lx plebi
\ph plébîʔ
\ps n
\pl aplebi
\plph aplébîʔ
\sn 1
\ge scar
\is 2.1.4
\is 7.9.1
\de scar
\xv Mɩa oyin anfɩ anɩlɔkɔ, sʋ nɛdɩnka mʋ plebi.
\xe This man and I we fought so I scarred him.
\xv Plebi dɩn mɩ owunfinsʋ ansɩtɔ.
\xe There is a scar on my forehead.
\ps n
\sn 2
\ge mark
\is 6.3.1
\is 7.7.7
\de to mark something in order to recognize it
\xv Oyin amʋ lɛha mʋ ɔbwɩ amʋ plebi, mɛnɩ owun mʋ a, ɔbɛ́bɩ mʋ.
\xe The man has marked his animal so that should he see it he will know it.
\cf hɩɛ nsʋ
\na There was a case where a farmer caught a man red-handed stealing yams from his farm. So he went to the family and complained. They said that because he had not cut him (kun) with a cutlass to mark him (dɩnka mʋ plebi) he had no proof that the accused was a thief. Therefore the family would not do anything. So the farmer has gone to the police to lodge a complaint and has said that if that is the case the next time the man comes to his farm to steal he will shoot him and if he kills him the family won't be able to say anything against him because this time they complained that he hadn't wounded him.
\dt 08/Sep/2016

\lx sɩmpʋa
\ph sɪmpʊa
\ps n
\va ɔnantswie sɩmpʋa
\vaph ɔnant͡swie sɪmpʊa
\sn
\ge boil
\is 2.5.2.2
\de cow boil
\ee At the time of the West African pound,  were used a threepence coin was sɩmpʋa. When these boils heal they leave small circular black scars and so take their name from the coin. They are differentiated from ordinary boils called kɛklɛ more by their outcome than by any physical characteristics. It is believed that if you inject someone who has a sɩmpʋa they will die. Recently a woman with severe hypertension developed a boil. As a logical extension of the above she went off her antihypertensives.
\xv Sɩmpʋa na ɔtsan ha kɛklɛ.
\xl Cow boil walks differently than kɛklɛ.
\xe Cow boils have different symptoms than boils.
\xv Nɩ ɩda fʋ a, igyi fɛ kɛklɛ tamɛ ɩtɛha fʋ ɩwɩ tɛblɩ ogya.
\xe If you get it, it is like a boil, but it makes your body hot.
\xv Nɩ fofuti a, ɩbɛ́talɩ mɔ fʋ.
\xe If you inject, it can kill you.
\xv Nnantswɩɛ ɛ bʋ tɔlɔ mʋ. Mʋ́ sʋ bʋtɛtɩ mʋ́ ɔnantswie sɩmpʋa nɩ.
\xe Cows also get these boils. Because of this they call it cow boil.
\dt 13/Jan/2017

\ps v
\sn 2

\de almost
\ng In this sense, ta occurs as the second in a serial verb construction.
\xv Olowu ta.
\xe He almost died.
\xv Nɩ oputsu ta atogyihɛtɔ a, ɩma agyilɛ.
\xe If the soup in the food finishes it is not easy to eat.

\xe He almost finished eating.

\xv Ɔlɛta wu ta.
\xe He very nearly died.
\xv Ɔlɛta gyi ta.
\xe He very nearly finished eating.
\ng This is an example of the grammatical form of fronting and repeating a verb for emphasis.

\lx yɔɩ
\ph yɔ́ɪ́
\ps v
\sn 1
\ge die
\is 2.6.6
\de to stop breathing
\cf lɛ ɔŋɛ
\cf wu
\xv Bɛda bubu bakɛ bɛɛ, Okuma dewu. Anɩlotsutsua wie ɩnʋ a, alayɔɩ.
\xe They called the alarm before dawn that Okuma is dying, before we reached there, she had stopped breathing.
\ps v
\sn 2
\ge digest
\is 2.1.8.2
\is 5.2.2
\de to digest food
\xv Nɩ fegyi atɔ nyankɩ a, owí botsutsua dan atogyihɛ amʋ bɔ́yɔɩ fʋ, fówun akʋn lala dɛ fʋ.
\xe When you eat in the morning, before noon the food will digest then you will be hungry again.
